<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Andes</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mountain range in South America</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the mountain range in South America. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Andes_(disambiguation)" title="Andes (disambiguation)">Andes (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cono_de_Arita,_Salta._(Argentina).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1353" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg/220px-Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg/330px-Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg/440px-Cono_de_Arita%2C_Salta._%28Argentina%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cono_de_Arita,_Salta._(Argentina).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Arizaro" title="Salar de Arizaro">Cono de Arita</a>" in the <a href="/wiki/Puna_de_Atacama" title="Puna de Atacama">Puna de Atacama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salta" title="Salta">Salta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg/220px-Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg/330px-Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg/440px-Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aconcagua_south_wall_2020.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aconcagua</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Andes</b>, <b>Andes Mountains</b> or <b>Andean Mountains</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Cordillera de los Andes</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_language" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>: <i lang="qu">Anti</i>) are the <a href="/wiki/List_of_mountain_ranges#Mountain_ranges_by_length" title="List of mountain ranges">longest continental mountain range</a> in the world, forming a continuous highland along the western edge of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>. The range is 8,900 km (5,530 mi) long, 200 to 700 km (124 to 435 mi) wide (widest between <a href="/wiki/18th_parallel_south" title="18th parallel south">18°S</a> – <a href="/wiki/20th_parallel_south" title="20th parallel south">20°S</a> <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>), and has an average height of about 4,000 m (13,123 ft). The Andes extend from north to south through seven South American countries: <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>.
</p><p>Along their length, the Andes are split into several ranges, separated by intermediate <a href="/wiki/Depression_(geology)" title="Depression (geology)">depressions</a>. The Andes are the location of several high <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateaus</a>—some of which host major cities such as <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cali" title="Cali">Cali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arequipa" title="Arequipa">Arequipa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medell%C3%ADn" title="Medellín">Medellín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bucaramanga" title="Bucaramanga">Bucaramanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sucre" title="Sucre">Sucre</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_M%C3%A9rida" title="Mérida, Mérida">Mérida</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Alto" title="El Alto">El Alto</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_Paz" title="La Paz">La Paz</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano plateau</a> is the world's second-highest after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_plateau" title="Tibetan plateau">Tibetan plateau</a>. These ranges are in turn grouped into three major divisions based on climate: the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Andes" title="Tropical Andes">Tropical Andes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dry_Andes" title="Dry Andes">Dry Andes</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wet_Andes" title="Wet Andes">Wet Andes</a>.
</p><p>The Andes Mountains are the highest mountain range outside Asia. The highest mountain outside Asia, Argentina's Mount <a href="/wiki/Aconcagua" title="Aconcagua">Aconcagua</a>, rises to an elevation of about 6,961 m (22,838 ft) above sea level. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summit_(topography)" title="Summit (topography)">peak</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimborazo_(volcano)" title="Chimborazo (volcano)">Chimborazo</a> in the Ecuadorian Andes is farther from the Earth's center than any other location on the Earth's surface, due to the <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">equatorial bulge</a> resulting from the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">Earth's rotation</a>. The world's highest <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> are in the Andes, including <a href="/wiki/Ojos_del_Salado" title="Ojos del Salado">Ojos del Salado</a> on the Chile-Argentina border, which rises to 6,893 m (22,615 ft).
</p><p>The Andes are also part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Cordillera" title="American Cordillera">American Cordillera</a>, a chain of mountain ranges (<a href="/wiki/Cordillera" title="Cordillera">cordillera</a>) that consists of an almost continuous sequence of mountain ranges that form the western "backbone" of North America, Central America, South America and Antarctica.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The etymology of the word <i>Andes</i> has been debated. The majority consensus is that it derives from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a> word <span title="Quechua-language text"><i lang="qu">anti</i></span> 'east'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laime_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-laime-1">[1]</a></sup> as in <i><a href="/wiki/Antisuyu" title="Antisuyu">Antisuyu</a></i> (Quechua for 'east region'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laime_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-laime-1">[1]</a></sup> one of the four regions of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>.
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Cordillera" title="Cordillera">cordillera</a></i> comes from the Spanish word <i>cordel</i> 'rope'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and is used as a descriptive name for several contiguous sections of the Andes, as well as the entire Andean range, and the combined mountain chain along the western part of the North and South American continents.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_(Ushuaia)%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4232" data-file-width="6335" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg/220px-ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg/330px-ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg/440px-ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_%28Ushuaia%29%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ARG-2016-Aerial-Tierra_del_Fuego_(Ushuaia)%E2%80%93Valle_Carbajal_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Carbajal_Valley" title="Carbajal Valley">Valle Carbajal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Fuegian</a> The Andes range is about 200 km (124 mi) wide throughout its length, except in the <a href="/wiki/Orocline" title="Orocline">Bolivian flexure</a> where it is about 640 kilometres (398 mi) wide.</div></div></div>
<p>The Andes can be divided into three sections:
</p>
<dl><dt>The Southern Andes</dt>
<dd>in Argentina and Chile, south of <a href="/wiki/Llullaillaco" title="Llullaillaco">Llullaillaco</a>.</dd>
<dt>The Central Andes</dt>
<dd>in Peru and Bolivia.</dd>
<dt>The Northern Andes</dt>
<dd>in Venezuela, Colombia, and Ecuador. In the northern part of the Andes, the separate <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_de_Santa_Marta" title="Sierra Nevada de Santa Marta">Sierra Nevada de Santa Marta</a> range is often treated as part of the Northern Andes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Leeward_Antilles" title="Leeward Antilles">Leeward Antilles</a> islands <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">Aruba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonaire" title="Bonaire">Bonaire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cura%C3%A7ao" title="Curaçao">Curaçao</a>, which lie in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> off the coast of Venezuela, were formerly thought to represent the submerged peaks of the extreme northern edge of the Andes range, but ongoing geological studies indicate that such a simplification does not do justice to the complex tectonic boundary between the South American and Caribbean plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><span>This box:  </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Geology_of_the_Andes" title="Template:Geology of the Andes"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Geology_of_the_Andes" title="Template talk:Geology of the Andes"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Geology_of_the_Andes&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>
<hr/>
<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1550" decoding="async" height="21" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/32px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/48px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/64px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg 2x" width="32"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Andes" title="Portal:Andes">Andes portal</a><br/>
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WikiProject_Geology.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="327" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WikiProject_Geology.svg/28px-WikiProject_Geology.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WikiProject_Geology.svg/42px-WikiProject_Geology.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/WikiProject_Geology.svg/56px-WikiProject_Geology.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Geology" title="Portal:Geology">Geology portal</a>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>The Andes are a <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a>–<a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orogenic" title="Orogenic">orogenic</a> belt of mountains along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Ring_of_Fire" title="Pacific Ring of Fire">Pacific Ring of Fire</a>, a zone of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_activity" title="Volcanic activity">volcanic activity</a> that encompasses the Pacific rim of the Americas as well as the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific" title="Asia-Pacific">Asia-Pacific</a> region. The Andes are the result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plate</a> processes, caused by the <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> beneath the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American Plate</a>. It is the result of a convergent plate boundary between the Nazca Plate and the South American Plate. The main cause of the rise of the Andes is the compression of the western rim of the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American Plate</a> due to the subduction of the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">Nazca Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a>. To the east, the Andes range is bounded by several <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_basin" title="Sedimentary basin">sedimentary basins</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Orinoco" title="Orinoco">Orinoco</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon Basin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madre_de_Dios_River" title="Madre de Dios River">Madre de Dios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gran_Chaco" title="Gran Chaco">Gran Chaco</a>, that separate the Andes from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a> in eastern South America. In the south, the Andes share a long boundary with the former <a href="/wiki/Patagonia#Geology" title="Patagonia">Patagonia Terrane</a>. To the west, the Andes end at the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, although the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peru-Chile_trench" title="Peru-Chile trench">Peru-Chile trench</a> can be considered their ultimate western limit. From a geographical approach, the Andes are considered to have their western boundaries marked by the appearance of coastal lowlands and a less rugged topography. The Andes Mountains also contain large quantities of iron ore located in many mountains within the range.
</p><p>The Andean orogen has a series of bends or <a href="/wiki/Orocline" title="Orocline">oroclines</a>. The Bolivian Orocline is a seaward concave bending in the coast of South America and the Andes Mountains at about 18° S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isacks1988_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isacks1988-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kley1999_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kley1999-6">[6]</a></sup> At this point, the orientation of the Andes turns from Northwest in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> to South in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kley1999_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kley1999-6">[6]</a></sup> The Andean segment north and south of the Orocline have been rotated 15° to 20° counter clockwise and clockwise respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kley1999_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kley1999-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck1987_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beck1987-7">[7]</a></sup> The Bolivian Orocline area overlaps with the area of maximum width of the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano Plateau</a> and according to Isacks (1988) the Orocline is related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustal_shortening" title="Crustal shortening">crustal shortening</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isacks1988_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isacks1988-5">[5]</a></sup> The specific point at 18° S where the coastline bends is known as the "Arica Elbow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PrezziVilas_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-PrezziVilas-8">[8]</a></sup> Further south lies the Maipo Orocline a more subtle Orocline between 30° S and 38°S with a seaward-concave break in trend at 33° S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arriagada2013_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arriagada2013-9">[9]</a></sup> Near the southern tip of the Andes lies the Patagonian Orocline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charrieretal2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charrieretal2006-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orogeny">Orogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Andean_orogeny" title="Andean orogeny">Andean orogeny</a></div>
<p>The western rim of the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American Plate</a> has been the place of several pre-Andean <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogenies</a> since at least the late <a href="/wiki/Proterozoic" title="Proterozoic">Proterozoic</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>, when several <a href="/wiki/Terrane" title="Terrane">terranes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microcontinent" title="Microcontinent">microcontinents</a> collided and amalgamated with the ancient <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a> of eastern South America, by then the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American part</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>.
</p><p>The formation of the modern Andes began with the events of the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> when <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> began the break up that resulted in developing several <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">rifts</a>. The development continued through the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> Period. It was during the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> Period that the Andes began to take their present form, by the <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">uplifting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folding</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> rocks of the ancient cratons to the east. The rise of the Andes has not been constant, as different regions have had different degrees of tectonic stress, uplift, and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>.
</p><p>Tectonic forces above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subduction_zone" title="Subduction zone">subduction zone</a> along the entire west coast of South America where the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">Nazca Plate</a> and a part of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a> are sliding beneath the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American Plate</a> continue to produce an ongoing <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogenic event</a> resulting in minor to major <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruptions</a> to this day. In the extreme south, a major <a href="/wiki/Transform_fault" title="Transform fault">transform fault</a> separates <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> from the small <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Plate" title="Scotia Plate">Scotia Plate</a>. Across the 1,000 km (620 mi) wide <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> lie the mountains of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">Antarctic Peninsula</a> south of the Scotia Plate which appear to be a continuation of the Andes chain.
</p><p>The regions immediately east of the Andes experience a series of changes resulting from the Andean orogeny. Parts of the <a href="/wiki/Suns%C3%A1s_orogeny" title="Sunsás orogeny">Sunsás Orogen</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazonian_craton" title="Amazonian craton">Amazonian craton</a> disappeared from the surface of earth being <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">overridden</a> by the Andes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Sierras_de_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Sierras de Córdoba">Sierras de Córdoba</a>, where the effects of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Pampean_orogeny" title="Pampean orogeny">Pampean orogeny</a> can be observed, owe their modern uplift and relief to the <a href="/wiki/Andean_orogeny" title="Andean orogeny">Andean orogeny</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rapelaetal1998_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rapelaetal1998-12">[12]</a></sup> Further south in southern <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> the onset of the Andean orogeny caused the <a href="/wiki/Magallanes_Basin" title="Magallanes Basin">Magallanes Basin</a> to evolve from being an <a href="/wiki/Extensional_tectonics" title="Extensional tectonics">extensional</a> <a href="/wiki/Back-arc_basin" title="Back-arc basin">back-arc basin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> to being a compressional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andean_Foreland_Basin" title="Andean Foreland Basin">foreland basin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">Volcanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Volcanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Andean_Volcanic_Belt" title="Andean Volcanic Belt">Andean Volcanic Belt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg/220px-Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg/330px-Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg/440px-Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Browncanyonquilotoa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">Rift valley</a> near <a href="/wiki/Quilotoa" title="Quilotoa">Quilotoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Central_Andes_Mountains,_Salar_de_Arizaro,_Argentina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4256" data-file-width="2800" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg/170px-Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg/255px-Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg/340px-Central_Andes_Mountains%2C_Salar_de_Arizaro%2C_Argentina.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Central_Andes_Mountains,_Salar_de_Arizaro,_Argentina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This photo from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISS" title="ISS">ISS</a> shows the high plains of the Andes Mountains in the foreground, with a line of young volcanoes facing the much lower Atacama Desert</div></div></div>
<p>The Andes range has many active volcanoes distributed in four volcanic zones separated by areas of inactivity. The Andean volcanism is a result of <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> of the Nazca Plate and Antarctic Plate underneath the South American Plate. The belt is subdivided into four main volcanic zones that are separated from each other by volcanic gaps. The volcanoes of the belt are diverse in terms of activity style, products and morphology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> While some differences can be explained by which volcanic zone a volcano belongs to, there are significant differences inside volcanic zones and even between neighbouring volcanoes. Despite being a type location for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calc-alkalic" title="Calc-alkalic">calc-alkalic</a> and subduction volcanism, the Andean Volcanic Belt has a large range of volcano-tectonic settings, such as rift systems and extensional zones, transpressional faults, subduction of <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamount</a> chains apart from a large range of crustal thicknesses and <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> ascent paths, and different amount of crustal assimilations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ore_deposits_and_evaporates">Ore deposits and evaporates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ore deposits and evaporates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Andes Mountains host large <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> deposits and some of their eastern <a href="/wiki/Fold_and_thrust_belt" title="Fold and thrust belt">fold and thrust belt</a> acts as traps for commercially exploitable amounts of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>. In the forelands of the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Desert" title="Atacama Desert">Atacama Desert</a> some of the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porphyry_copper" title="Porphyry copper">porphyry copper</a> mineralizations occur making Chile and Peru the first- and second-largest exporters of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> in the world. Porphyry copper in the western slopes of the Andes has been generated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_fluid" title="Hydrothermal fluid">hydrothermal fluids</a> (mostly water) during the cooling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluton_(geology)" title="Pluton (geology)">plutons</a> or volcanic systems. The porphyry mineralization further benefited from the dry climate that let them largely out of the disturbing actions of <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_water" title="Meteoric water">meteoric water</a>. The dry climate in the central western Andes has also led to the creation of extensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chile_saltpeter" title="Chile saltpeter">saltpeter deposits</a> which were extensively mined until the invention of synthetic nitrates. Yet another result of the dry climate are the <a href="/wiki/Dry_lake" title="Dry lake">salars</a> of <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Atacama" title="Salar de Atacama">Atacama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Uyuni</a>, the first one being the largest source of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> today and the second the world's largest reserve of the element. Early Mesozoic and <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> plutonism in Bolivia's Cordillera Central created the Bolivian <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> belt as well as the famous, now depleted, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Cerro_Rico" title="Cerro Rico">Cerro Rico de Potosí</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Andean_South_America" title="History of Andean South America">History of Andean South America</a></div>
<p>The Andes Mountains, initially inhabited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>, experienced the development of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and rise of politically centralised <a href="/wiki/Andean_civilizations" title="Andean civilizations">civilizations</a>, which culminated in the establishment of the century-long <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>. This all changed in the 16th century, when the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">conquistadors</a> colonized the mountains in the advance of the <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> economy.
</p><p>In tide of anti-imperialist nationalism, the Andes became the scene of a <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">series of independence wars</a> in the 19th century when rebel forces swept through the region to overthrow Spanish colonial rule. Since then, many former Spanish territories have become five independent Andean states.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_and_hydrology">Climate and hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate and hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Andes" title="Tropical Andes">Tropical Andes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dry_Andes" title="Dry Andes">Dry Andes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wet_Andes" title="Wet Andes">Wet Andes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andes1a.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="1380" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Andes1a.JPG/220px-Andes1a.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Andes1a.JPG/330px-Andes1a.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Andes1a.JPG/440px-Andes1a.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andes1a.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Central Andes</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andes_bolivianos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Andes_bolivianos.jpg/220px-Andes_bolivianos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Andes_bolivianos.jpg/330px-Andes_bolivianos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Andes_bolivianos.jpg/440px-Andes_bolivianos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andes_bolivianos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bolivian Andes</div></div></div>
<p>The climate in the Andes varies greatly depending on latitude, altitude, and proximity to the sea. Temperature, atmospheric pressure and humidity decrease in higher elevations. The southern section is rainy and cool, the central section is dry. The northern Andes are typically rainy and warm, with an average temperature of 18 °C (64 °F) in Colombia. The climate is known to change drastically in rather short distances. <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">Rainforests</a> exist just kilometres away from the snow-covered peak <a href="/wiki/Cotopaxi" title="Cotopaxi">Cotopaxi</a>. The mountains have a large effect on the temperatures of nearby areas. The <a href="/wiki/Snow_line" title="Snow line">snow line</a> depends on the location. It is at between 4,500 and 4,800 m (14,764 and 15,748 ft) in the tropical Ecuadorian, Colombian, Venezuelan, and northern Peruvian Andes, rising to 4,800–5,200 m (15,748–17,060 ft) in the drier mountains of southern Peru south to northern Chile south to about <a href="/wiki/30th_parallel_south" title="30th parallel south">30°S</a> before descending to 4,500 m (14,760 ft) on Aconcagua at <a href="/wiki/32nd_parallel_south" title="32nd parallel south">32°S</a>, 2,000 m (6,600 ft) at <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40°S</a>, 500 m (1,640 ft) at <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50°S</a>, and only 300 m (980 ft) in <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> at <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55°S</a>; from 50°S, several of the larger glaciers descend to sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Andes of Chile and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> can be divided into two climatic and glaciological zones: the <a href="/wiki/Dry_Andes" title="Dry Andes">Dry Andes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wet_Andes" title="Wet Andes">Wet Andes</a>. Since the Dry Andes extend from the latitudes of <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Desert" title="Atacama Desert">Atacama Desert</a> to the area of <a href="/wiki/Maule_River" title="Maule River">Maule River</a>, precipitation is more sporadic and there are strong temperature oscillations. The line of equilibrium may shift drastically over short periods of time, leaving a whole glacier in the <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">ablation</a> area or in the <a href="/wiki/Glacier_ice_accumulation" title="Glacier ice accumulation">accumulation area</a>.
</p><p>In the high Andes of <a href="/wiki/Central_Chile" title="Central Chile">Central Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mendoza_Province" title="Mendoza Province">Mendoza Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_glacier" title="Rock glacier">rock glaciers</a> are larger and more common than glaciers; this is due to the high exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In these regions glaciers occur typically at higher altitudes than rock glaciers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corte1976_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corte1976-17">[17]</a></sup> The lowest active rock glacier occur at 900 m a.s.l. in <a href="/wiki/Aconcagua" title="Aconcagua">Aconcagua</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corte1976_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corte1976-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though precipitation increases with the height, there are semiarid conditions in the nearly 7,000-metre (22,966 ft) highest mountains of the Andes. This dry steppe climate is considered to be typical of the subtropical position at 32–34° S. The valley bottoms have no woods, just dwarf scrub. The largest glaciers, for example the Plomo glacier and the Horcones glaciers, do not even reach 10 km (6.2 mi) in length and have an only insignificant ice thickness. At glacial times, however, c. 20,000 years ago, the glaciers were over ten times longer. On the east side of this section of the Mendozina Andes, they flowed down to 2,060 m (6,759 ft) and on the west side to about 1,220 m (4,003 ft) above sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle,_M._2011_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle,_M._2011-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The massifs of Cerro Aconcagua (6,961 m (22,838 ft)), Cerro Tupungato (6,550 m (21,490 ft)) and Nevado Juncal (6,110 m (20,046 ft)) are tens of kilometres away from each other and were connected by a joint ice stream network. The Andes' dendritic glacier arms, i.e. components of valley glaciers, were up to 112.5 km (69.9 mi) long, over 1,250 m (4,101 ft) thick and overspanned a vertical distance of 5,150 m (16,896 ft). The climatic glacier snowline (ELA) was lowered from 4,600 m (15,092 ft) to 3,200 m (10,499 ft) at glacial times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle,_M._2011_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle,_M._2011-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20100116_Sonso_002.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="1014" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/20100116_Sonso_002.JPG/220px-20100116_Sonso_002.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/20100116_Sonso_002.JPG/330px-20100116_Sonso_002.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/20100116_Sonso_002.JPG/440px-20100116_Sonso_002.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20100116_Sonso_002.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Laguna_de_Sonso_Nature_Reserve" title="Laguna de Sonso Nature Reserve">Laguna de Sonso</a> tropical dry forest in Northern Andes</div></div></div>
<p>The Andean region cuts across several <a href="/wiki/Natural_region" title="Natural region">natural</a> and floristic regions, due to its extension, from <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> Venezuela to cold, windy and wet <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> passing through the hyperarid Atacama Desert. <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">Rainforests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_dry_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical dry broadleaf forests">tropical dry forests</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> used to encircle much of the northern Andes but are now greatly <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">diminished</a>, especially in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Choc%C3%B3" title="El Chocó">Chocó</a> and inter-Andean valleys of Colombia. Opposite of the humid Andean slopes are the relatively dry Andean slopes in most of western Peru, Chile and Argentina. Along with several <a href="/wiki/Interandean_Valles" title="Interandean Valles">Interandean Valles</a>, they are typically dominated by <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> woodland, shrub and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xeric" title="Xeric">xeric</a> vegetation, reaching the extreme in the slopes near the virtually lifeless Atacama Desert.
</p><p>About 30,000 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_plants" title="Vascular plants">vascular plants</a> live in the Andes, with roughly half being <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to the region, surpassing the diversity of any other <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">hotspot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiv_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-biodiv-29">[29]</a></sup> The small tree <i><a href="/wiki/Cinchona_pubescens" title="Cinchona pubescens">Cinchona pubescens</a></i>, a source of <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a> which is used to treat <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, is found widely in the Andes as far south as Bolivia. Other important crops that originated from the Andes are <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>. The high-altitude <i><a href="/wiki/Polylepis" title="Polylepis">Polylepis</a></i> forests and woodlands are found in the Andean areas of Colombia, Ecuador, Peru, Bolivia and Chile. These trees, by locals referred to as Queñua, Yagual and other names, can be found at altitudes of 4,500 m (14,760 ft) above sea level. It remains unclear if the patchy distribution of these forests and woodlands is natural, or the result of clearing which began during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Incan</a> period. Regardless, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">modern times</a> the clearance has accelerated, and the trees are now considered to be highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a>, with some believing that as little as 10% of the original woodland remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg/220px-Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg/330px-Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg/440px-Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tunki_Tanpupata.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A male <a href="/wiki/Andean_cock-of-the-rock" title="Andean cock-of-the-rock">Andean cock-of-the-rock</a>, a species found in humid Andean forests and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_bird" title="National bird">national bird</a> of Peru</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1550" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/220px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/330px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg/440px-Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ausangate-hillside-MT.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Herds of <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a> near <a href="/wiki/Ausangate" title="Ausangate">Ausangate</a> mountain</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_the_Andes" title="Fauna of the Andes">Fauna of the Andes</a></div>
<p>The Andes are rich in fauna: With almost 1,000 species, of which roughly 2/3 are <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to the region, the Andes are the most important region in the world for <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiv_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-biodiv-29">[29]</a></sup>
The diversity of animals in the Andes is high, with almost 600 species of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> (13% endemic), more than 1,700 species of birds (about 1/3 endemic), more than 600 species of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptile</a> (about 45% endemic), and almost 400 species of fish (about 1/3 endemic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiv_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-biodiv-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vicu%C3%B1a" title="Vicuña">vicuña</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guanaco" title="Guanaco">guanaco</a> can be found living in the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a>, while the closely related <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a> are widely kept by locals as <a href="/wiki/Pack_animal" title="Pack animal">pack animals</a> and for their <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>. The crepuscular (active during dawn and dusk) <a href="/wiki/Chinchilla" title="Chinchilla">chinchillas</a>, two threatened members of the <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> order, inhabit the Andes' alpine regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoMammals3_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NeoMammals3-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoMammals2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NeoMammals2-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Andean_condor" title="Andean condor">Andean condor</a>, the largest bird of its kind in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>, occurs throughout much of the Andes but generally in very low densities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup> Other animals found in the relatively open habitats of the high Andes include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huemul_(zoology)" title="Huemul (zoology)">huemul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">cougar</a>, foxes in the genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudalopex" title="Pseudalopex">Pseudalopex</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoMammals3_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-NeoMammals3-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoMammals2_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-NeoMammals2-32">[32]</a></sup> and, for birds, certain species of <a href="/wiki/Tinamou" title="Tinamou">tinamous</a> (notably members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Nothoprocta" title="Nothoprocta">Nothoprocta</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Andean_goose" title="Andean goose">Andean goose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giant_coot" title="Giant coot">giant coot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">flamingos</a> (mainly associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypersaline" title="Hypersaline">hypersaline</a> lakes), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesser_rhea" title="Lesser rhea">lesser rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andean_flicker" title="Andean flicker">Andean flicker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diademed_sandpiper-plover" title="Diademed sandpiper-plover">diademed sandpiper-plover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geositta" title="Geositta">miners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phrygilus" title="Phrygilus">sierra-finches</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diuca" title="Diuca">diuca-finches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> hosts several endemics, among them the highly endangered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titicaca_flightless_grebe" title="Titicaca flightless grebe">Titicaca flightless grebe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titicaca_water_frog" title="Titicaca water frog">Titicaca water frog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreatenedAmph_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThreatenedAmph-34">[34]</a></sup> A few species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hummingbirds" title="Hummingbirds">hummingbirds</a>, notably some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oreotrochilus" title="Oreotrochilus">hillstars</a>, can be seen at altitudes above 4,000 m (13,100 ft), but far higher <a href="/wiki/Species_richness" title="Species richness">diversities</a> can be found at lower altitudes, especially in the humid Andean forests ("<a href="/wiki/Cloud_forest" title="Cloud forest">cloud forests</a>") growing on slopes in Colombia, Ecuador, Peru, Bolivia and far northwestern Argentina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup> These forest-types, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Yungas" title="Yungas">Yungas</a> and parts of the Chocó, are very rich in flora and fauna, although few large mammals exist, exceptions being the threatened <a href="/wiki/Mountain_tapir" title="Mountain tapir">mountain tapir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spectacled_bear" title="Spectacled bear">spectacled bear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow-tailed_woolly_monkey" title="Yellow-tailed woolly monkey">yellow-tailed woolly monkey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeoMammals3_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-NeoMammals3-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Birds of humid Andean forests include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain-toucan" title="Mountain-toucan">mountain-toucans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quetzal" title="Quetzal">quetzals</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Andean_cock-of-the-rock" title="Andean cock-of-the-rock">Andean cock-of-the-rock</a>, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_species_flock" title="Mixed species flock">mixed species flocks</a> dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanagers" title="Tanagers">tanagers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Furnariids" title="Furnariids">furnariids</a> commonly are seen – in contrast to several vocal but typically <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">cryptic</a> species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrens" title="Wrens">wrens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tapaculo" title="Tapaculo">tapaculos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antpitta" title="Antpitta">antpittas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of species such as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_cinclodes" title="Royal cinclodes">royal cinclodes</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-browed_tit-spinetail" title="White-browed tit-spinetail">white-browed tit-spinetail</a> are associated with <i>Polylepis</i>, and consequently also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Threatened" title="Threatened">threatened</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HAndesBirds_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-HAndesBirds-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_activity">Human activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Human activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_periods_of_Peru" title="Cultural periods of Peru">Cultural periods of Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Viceroyalty of Peru</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andean_states" title="Andean states">Andean states</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2011</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Andes Mountains form a north–south axis of cultural influences. A long series of cultural development culminated in the expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> in the central Andes during the 15th century. The Incas formed this civilization through <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarism</a> as well as careful and meticulous governmental management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The government sponsored the construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(watercourse)" title="Aqueduct (watercourse)">aqueducts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roads" title="Roads">roads</a> in addition to preexisting installations. Some of these constructions are still in existence today.
</p><p>Devastated by European diseases and by <a href="/wiki/Inca_Civil_War" title="Inca Civil War">civil war</a>, the Incas were defeated in 1532 by an alliance composed of tens of thousands of allies from nations they had subjugated (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huanca_people" title="Huanca people">Huancas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chachapoyas_culture" title="Chachapoyas culture">Chachapoyas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ari" title="Cañari">Cañaris</a>) and a small army of 180 Spaniards led by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Pizarro" title="Francisco Pizarro">Francisco Pizarro</a>. One of the few Inca sites the Spanish never found in their conquest was <a href="/wiki/Machu_Picchu" title="Machu Picchu">Machu Picchu</a>, which lay hidden on a peak on the eastern edge of the Andes where they descend to the Amazon. The main surviving languages of the Andean peoples are those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">Aymara language</a> families. <a href="/wiki/Woodbine_Parish" title="Woodbine Parish">Woodbine Parish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barclay_Pentland" title="Joseph Barclay Pentland">Joseph Barclay Pentland</a> surveyed a large part of the Bolivian Andes from 1826 to 1827.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cities">Cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In modern times, the largest cities in the Andes are <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, with a population of about eight million, <a href="/wiki/Santiago" title="Santiago">Santiago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medell%C3%ADn" title="Medellín">Medellín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cali" title="Cali">Cali</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a> is a coastal city adjacent to the Andes and is the largest city of all Andean countries. It is the seat of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andean_Community_of_Nations" title="Andean Community of Nations">Andean Community of Nations</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/La_Paz" title="La Paz">La Paz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>'s seat of government, is the highest capital city in the world, at an elevation of approximately 3,650 m (11,975 ft). Parts of the La Paz conurbation, including the city of <a href="/wiki/El_Alto" title="El Alto">El Alto</a>, extend up to 4,200 m (13,780 ft).
</p><p>Other cities in or near the Andes include <a href="/wiki/Bariloche" title="Bariloche">Bariloche</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Fernando_del_Valle_de_Catamarca" title="San Fernando del Valle de Catamarca">Catamarca</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador_de_Jujuy" title="San Salvador de Jujuy">Jujuy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mendoza,_Argentina" title="Mendoza, Argentina">Mendoza</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salta,_Argentina" title="Salta, Argentina">Salta</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Argentina" title="San Juan, Argentina">San Juan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/San_Miguel_de_Tucum%C3%A1n" title="San Miguel de Tucumán">Tucumán</a> in Argentina; <a href="/wiki/Calama,_Chile" title="Calama, Chile">Calama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rancagua" title="Rancagua">Rancagua</a> in Chile; <a href="/wiki/Cochabamba" title="Cochabamba">Cochabamba</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oruro,_Bolivia" title="Oruro, Bolivia">Oruro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potos%C3%AD" title="Potosí">Potosí</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sucre" title="Sucre">Sucre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sacaba" title="Sacaba">Sacaba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarija" title="Tarija">Tarija</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yacuiba" title="Yacuiba">Yacuiba</a> in Bolivia; <a href="/wiki/Arequipa" title="Arequipa">Arequipa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cajamarca" title="Cajamarca">Cajamarca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huancayo" title="Huancayo">Huancayo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hu%C3%A1nuco" title="Huánuco">Huánuco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huaraz" title="Huaraz">Huaraz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juliaca" title="Juliaca">Juliaca</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Puno" title="Puno">Puno</a> in Peru; <a href="/wiki/Ambato,_Ecuador" title="Ambato, Ecuador">Ambato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuenca,_Ecuador" title="Cuenca, Ecuador">Cuenca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibarra,_Ecuador" title="Ibarra, Ecuador">Ibarra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latacunga" title="Latacunga">Latacunga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loja,_Ecuador" title="Loja, Ecuador">Loja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riobamba" title="Riobamba">Riobamba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tulc%C3%A1n" title="Tulcán">Tulcán</a> in Ecuador; <a href="/wiki/Armenia,_Colombia" title="Armenia, Colombia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/C%C3%BAcuta" title="Cúcuta">Cúcuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bucaramanga" title="Bucaramanga">Bucaramanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duitama" title="Duitama">Duitama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibagu%C3%A9" title="Ibagué">Ibagué</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ipiales" title="Ipiales">Ipiales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manizales" title="Manizales">Manizales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmira,_Valle_del_Cauca" title="Palmira, Valle del Cauca">Palmira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasto,_Colombia" title="Pasto, Colombia">Pasto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pereira,_Colombia" title="Pereira, Colombia">Pereira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Popay%C3%A1n" title="Popayán">Popayán</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogamoso" title="Sogamoso">Sogamoso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunja" title="Tunja">Tunja</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Villavicencio" title="Villavicencio">Villavicencio</a> in Colombia; and <a href="/wiki/Barquisimeto" title="Barquisimeto">Barquisimeto</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Grita" title="La Grita">La Grita</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_M%C3%A9rida" title="Mérida, Mérida">Mérida</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal,_T%C3%A1chira" title="San Cristóbal, Táchira">San Cristóbal</a>, Tovar, <a href="/wiki/Trujillo,_Trujillo" title="Trujillo, Trujillo">Trujillo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Valera" title="Valera">Valera</a> in Venezuela. The cities of <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valencia,_Carabobo" title="Valencia, Carabobo">Valencia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maracay" title="Maracay">Maracay</a> are in the <a href="/wiki/Venezuelan_Coastal_Range" title="Venezuelan Coastal Range">Venezuelan Coastal Range</a>, which is a debatable extension of the Andes at the northern extremity of South America.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1021810730">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-default{background:transparent;margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-center{margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-none{float:none}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-collapsible{width:100%}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:center;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main>div{display:table-cell}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallery{line-height:1.35em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:right;font-size:80%;line-height:1em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div *,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div *{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallerybox img{background:none!important}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .bordered-images .thumb img{border:solid #eaecf0 1px}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .whitebg .thumb{background:#fff!important}</style><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">
